        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202411531')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="077166b30a804fc29e833af865897bcc">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '4e5a0cbd-dd9a-4219-a61e-05f97de4066c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d22396731" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Rationally designing the electrolyte system toward improving the electrochemical performance, especially the rate capability, of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) is very important for accelerating their large-scale commercialization. Herein, it is shown that by refining the molar ratio of two ether solvents, namely dimethoxyethane (DME) and 2-methyl tetrahydrofuran (MeTHF), a binary solvent electrolyte system forms a sodium ion solvation structure that facilitates high rate charge/discharge of hard carbon (HC) electrodes. It is demonstrated that the boosted rate capability can be attributed to the enhanced sodium ion transportation and desolvation kinetics, resulting from the participation of weak-coordinating MeTHF molecule with low steric hindrance in the sodium ion solvation sheath, which weakens the interaction between sodium ion and solvent molecules/anions through electron density regulation at the bond critical points (BCPs). The thin and uniform solid electrolyte interphase film on HC electrodes formed in such an ether-based electrolyte is also beneficial for improving the rate performance and cycling stability. The results of the present study shed more light on how the electron density engineering at the BCPs in sodium ion solvation sheath affects the rate capability of HC electrodes and promote its practical application prospect in future sodium-based battery chemistries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202411531-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202411531-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Along with the explosive development of electric vehicles and portable consumer electronics, lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely employed as the most successful electrochemical energy storage devices in the past decades.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0001_R_d22396723e368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Renew. Sust. Energ. Rev.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the meanwhile, however, the rapid growth of the large-scale energy storage market arises the concerns about the limited lithium resources and their geographically uneven distribution in the Earth's crust. In this context, sodium-based batteries have emerged as a fantastic alternative for the beyond-lithium electrochemical energy storage technology owing to the natural abundance of sodium element and their environmentally benign nature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0002_R_d22396723e373" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Storage Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with the maturely-developed commercial LIBs, the main components of sodium ion batteries (SIBs), including cathode and anode materials, electrolyte, separator, binder, and current collector, still need further investigation until approaching a practical industrial application level.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0003_R_d22396723e378" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In terms of anode materials, it is well-known that the conventional graphite is not suitable for use as the anode material in SIBs since the Na-graphite compounds with high Na contents are not thermodynamically stable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0004_R_d22396723e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. U.S.A.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, it is a hard task to search for suitable kinds of anode materials that can properly accommodate sodium ions and allow for their reversible intercalation/deintercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0005_R_d22396723e389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Compos. Hybrid Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Up to now, various types of materials, such as hard carbon (HC),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0006_R_d22396723e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> soft carbon,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0007_R_d22396723e400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Energy">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> transition metal sulfides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0008_R_d22396723e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been employed as potential anode materials for SIBs. Among them, HC is widely recognized as the most promising anode candidate for SIBs owing to its multiple advantages such as disordered structures, large interlayer spacing, higher specific capacity, low working potential, excellent electrical conductivity, resource abundance and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0009_R_d22396723e411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As such, great efforts have been devoted to synthesizing HC materials by using various kinds of raw materials such as phenolic resin,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0010_R_d22396723e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> biomass precursors such as pitch,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0011_R_d22396723e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Carbon">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> bamboo,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0012_R_d22396723e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> peanut shells,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0013_R_d22396723e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Energy Storage">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as well as optimizing the HC bulk structure, including heteroatom doping,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0014_R_d22396723e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> tuning closed pores,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0015_R_d22396723e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Energy Chem.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> pore size,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0016_R_d22396723e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> graphite sheet angle,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0017_R_d22396723e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and microstructure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0018_R_d22396723e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon Energy">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> for maximizing the specific capacity, initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) and cycling performance.</p>
         
         <p>It is well known that, besides the factors related to the anode material itself, the electrolyte also plays a very important role in improving the electrochemical performance for at least three reasons: (i) the electrolyte system determines the effective concentration of sodium ions as well as their mobility which regulate the sodium ion conductivity, (ii) the decomposition of electrolyte dictates the composition of the anode/electrolyte interphase (SEI) which adjusts the charge transfer kinetics, (iii) the composition of electrolyte governs the interaction among solvent molecules, anions and sodium ions, which influences the desolvation energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-anion complex during the interfacial charge transfer process. Therefore, electrolyte engineering is regarded as a powerful approach for boosting the electrochemical performance, especially the ICE and rate capability, of HC anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0019_R_d22396723e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater. ACS Nano Electrochim. Acta Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev. Nano Lett. Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, in terms of choosing the most suitable kinds of organic solvents for electrolyte design, organic solvents with high dielectric constants, low viscosity, and moderate Lewis acidity/basicity are important for favorable sodium salt dissolution and sodium ion conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0020_R_d22396723e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Sci. ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally speaking, organic solvents with high dielectric constants could induce solvent-separated ion pairs (SSIPs) solvation structures, which usually results in the formation of SEI films rich in organic species,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0020_R_d22396723e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Sci. ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while contact ion pairs (CIPs) or aggregates (AGGs) could lead to the formation of SEI films composed of inorganic components such as NaF facilitating sodium ion diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0020_R_d22396723e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Sci. ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Previous studies have shown that ether solvents exhibit promising prospects in electrolyte engineering since they generally have relatively higher lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy levels than those of conventional carbonate ester solvents,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0020_R_d22396723e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Sci. ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which effectively relieves the reduction reaction of solvent molecules and leads to the formation of a much thinner and an inorganic-rich SEI film derived from anion decomposition on HC electrode surface, resulting in significantly improved ICE and interfacial charge transfer kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0021_R_d22396723e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Dong et&nbsp;al. matched a nanospheric HC electrode with an ether (DEGDME)-based electrolyte and obtained a high ICE of 84.93% at a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as well as an excellent rate capability of 214 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0022_R_d22396723e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lv et&nbsp;al. demonstrated that a self-supporting additive-free porous HC electrode could deliver an outstanding rate performance of 288.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> coupled with a high reversible capacity of 272.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 700 cycles (187 days) when married with an ether (DME or DEGDME)-based electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0023_R_d22396723e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Eng. J. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Taken overall, up to now, the superior kinetics of HC electrodes in ether-based electrolytes have been clarified, however, the fundamental knowledge regarding the chemistry-property-performance relationship of the ether-based electrolyte is still unclear at the present time, and thus it is still a question and worth further exploration of how to rationally design an ether-based electrolyte system for high-performance SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Inspired by the above consideration, in this work, we demonstrate that by delicately refining the chemical composition and electrolyte solvation structure of the ether-based electrolyte, it is capable to obtain an HC electrode with excellent rate performance. The electrolyte is composed of 1&nbsp;M sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in a solvent mixture of dimethoxyethane (DME) and 2-methyl tetrahydrofuran (MeTHF) with an optimal molar ratio of 8:2, which is denoted as DM82. In term of the choice of organic solvent, DME was selected as the primary solvent owing to its decomposition products can help form a compact SEI on the carbon surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0023_R_d22396723e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Eng. J. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, considering that the relatively high viscosity of DME may limit its applicability at higher current densities, MeTHF was used as the co-solvent adding into the electrolyte system in order to reduce the overall viscosity of the solvent mixture and thus improve the rate capability of SIBs. MeTHF was screened out due to not only its relatively lower viscosity, but also the weaker solvation characteristics and environment-friendliness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0023_R_d22396723e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Eng. J. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The DM82-based electrolyte system enables the commercial HC electrode to yield a reversible capacity of 305 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, as well as a high ICE of 88.42% and a superior cycling performance (86.2% of the initial capacity remains after 500 charge/discharge cycles at 1 C (300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>)). Additionally, when sodium chromite (NaCrO<sub>2</sub>) is employed as the cathode material in a HC|NaCrO<sub>2</sub> full cell system, the full cell using DM82 as the electrolyte system retains 76.83% of its initial capacity after 200 cycles at a current density of 12.5&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. In comparison, the full cell using a commercially available ester-based electrolyte displays a sharp capacity decay during the first 10 cycles, and successively declines to 41.79% of its initial capacity after 200 cycles. In order to decipher the underlying molecular mechanism and to clarify how the molecular interactions regulate the electrochemical performance, classical molecular dynamics (CMD) simulation, density functional theory (DFT) calculations, electron structure analysis and spectroscopic characterization were jointly conducted, revealing that the high sodium ion diffusion coefficient and low sodium ion desolvation energy barrier are the underlying reasons for the boosted rate capability of HC electrodes with the ether-based electrolyte system DM82, resulting from the participation of weak-coordinating MeTHF molecule with low steric hindrance in the sodium ion solvation sheath, which properly weakens the molecular interaction in the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> complex through electron density regulation at bond critical points (BCPs). All in all, these discoveries and knowledge could shed more light on the potential application prospect of ether-based electrolytes and lay a solid foundation for further electrolyte engineering toward high-rate SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202411531-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202411531-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>From a theoretical point of view, the differences in redox reaction activities among different organic solvents were analysed based on the frontier molecular orbital theory associated with the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) and the LUMO energy levels, which could help to construct a preliminary view on the stability of electrolyte systems, providing a basic guidance for electrolyte formula design. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0001">1A</a>, it can be seen that the ether solvents (DME and MeTHF) have higher LUMO energy levels than those of ester solvents (ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC)), indicating that the ether solvents possess better reductive stability against anodes in comparison with the ester solvents. Therefore, generally speaking, a relatively rough SEI film mainly composed of organic products derived from aggressive solvent decomposition would be formed on HC anodes in ester-based electrolytes, which usually degrades the overall electrochemical performance of SIBs, while a smooth and compact inorganic-rich SEI film derived from anion decomposition could be generated on HC anodes in ether-based electrolytes, which is beneficial to stabilize the electrochemical performance of SIBs. On the basis of these analysis, herein, four different electrolyte systems containing 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME (hereafter denoted as D1) or MeTHF (hereafter denoted as M1), or both (hereafter denoted as DMXY, X:Y represents the molar ratio of DME to MeTHF), as well as a commercially available ester-based electrolyte composed of 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a solvent mixture of EC and DMC with a volume ratio of 1:1 (hereafter denoted as ED11), were prepared and used to assemble sodium ion coin cells for a comparison study, aiming to explore factors that can improve the electrochemical performance, especially the rate capability, of HC-based SIBs. First, we optimized the composition of the solvent mixture of DME and MeTHF through comprehensively characterizing the electrochemical performance, such as ICE, reversible capacity, and rate capability, of Na|HC half cells assembled using DMXY as the electrolyte system, with the molar ratio of DME to MeTHF ranging from 9:1 to 1:9. As shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the cells with electrolyte system DM82 exhibit relatively superior electrochemical performance comprehensively, including a large reversible capacity and a high ICE (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), especially the best rate capability (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), in comparison with those cells with other electrolyte systems. Therefore, in the following sections, we will systematically characterize the electrochemical performance and unravel the underlying molecular mechanism for the performance discrepancy of these cells with the four electrolyte system, namely D1, M1, DM82, and ED11.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202411531-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/35487eef-0997-4a05-b4c8-5bdb777eb860/smll202411531-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/35487eef-0997-4a05-b4c8-5bdb777eb860/smll202411531-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/411ceb1d-23a2-4960-96de-4bb5372bce15/smll202411531-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/35487eef-0997-4a05-b4c8-5bdb777eb860/smll202411531-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411531-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">HOMO-LUMO energy levels for different kinds of organic solvent molecules A), CV profiles B), GCD profiles C), specific capacity/ICE D), rate capabilities E), cycling performance F) of HC electrodes in different electrolyte system, GCD profiles of HC electrodes at various current densities with DM82 G) and ED11 H), respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Electrochemical behaviors of these four electrolyte systems were first analyzed by Na|HC half-cell experiments. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the first cycle at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> for Na|HC half cells with different electrolyte systems are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0001">1B</a>. A weak reduction peak appearing ≈1.0&nbsp;V during the first cycle is ascribed to a part of electrolyte decomposition forming an SEI film on HC electrodes, while the redox peaks displayed ≈0.1&nbsp;V indicate a reversible intercalation/extraction behavior of sodium ion in HC electrodes. Meanwhile, the positive/negative shift of the onset reduction/oxidation potential and an increase of the peak current amplitude ≈0.1&nbsp;V indicate a kinetics improvement of HC electrodes with different electrolyte systems in an ascending order of ED11, M1, D1, and DM82. Correspondingly, the first galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0001">1C</a>. All of these curves consist of a sloping region and a plateau region divided at ≈0.1&nbsp;V. The specific discharge capacity and ICE of HC electrodes were 350 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 84.94%, 359 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 86.3%, 346 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 88.42% and 369 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 83.39% for the electrolyte systems D1, M1, DM82, and ED11, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0001">1D</a>), revealing that the electrolyte system DM82 exhibits the highest ICE. Furthermore, the rate performance of the four electrolyte systems at different current densities is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0001">1E</a>. The rate performance significantly deteriorates with the increasing current densities for the ester-based electrolyte ED11 (332.0, 283.5, 215.8, 87.3, 71.8, 55.1, 39.6, and 13.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, and 20 C, respectively). In comparison, the ether-based electrolyte system DM82 exhibits a most superior rate capacity of 313.2, 291.6, 276.7, 266.0, 253.4, 223.7, 136.8, and 56.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, 10, and 20 C, respectively. More impressively, the HC electrode also exhibits more outstanding cycling stability in the ether-based electrolyte system DM82, delivering a high reversible capacity of 247.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 86.2% at 1 C after 500 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0001">1F</a>). In contrast, the HC electrode only maintains a reversible capacity of 72.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 74.9% at 1 C after 500 cycles in the ester-based electrolyte system ED11, revealing a significantly enhanced cycling performance for HC electrodes in ether-based electrolyte systems. In terms of rate capability and cycling stability, the cells with DM82 in this study deliver a moderate electrochemical performance among the reported cells with ether-based electrolyte systems (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0001">1G,H</a>, the plateau and slope capacity maintains 149.4 and 75.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 76.6% and 62.7% at 5 C, respectively, in the electrolyte system DM82 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0001">1G</a>). In comparison, the plateau capacity presents a sharp decline from 180.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C to 0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C while the slope capacity drops from 130.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C to 55.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C in the electrolyte system ED11 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0001">1H</a>). Meanwhile, as shown in the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), the sodium ion diffusion coefficients in the plateau and slope region are in the same level for the electrolyte system DM82, while the sodium ion diffusion coefficients in the plateau region are obviously much lower than those in the slope region for the electrolyte system ED11. These results indicate that the obvious plateau capacity fade associated with the sluggish sodium ion diffusion is the main cause for the inferior rate performance in electrolyte system ED11 compared to DM82.</p>
         
         <p>In order to examine the impact of electrolytes on the cycling stability of HC electrodes, the surface morphology evolution of HC electrodes before and after cycling in different electrolyte systems was first characterized by SEM characterization. For comparison, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4A,B</a> (Supporting Information) display the surface morphology of the pristine commercial HC powders, revealing an irregular shape of the HC particles with a wide size distribution ranging from sub-micrometer to several micrometers. Meanwhile, the particles have very smooth surface. The SEM images of the pristine HC electrode surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4C,D</a>, Supporting Information) show an interconnected network of active HC particles, conductive carbon black, and polymeric binder. After 200 discharge/charge cycles at a current density of 600&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, there are no notable differences in surface morphology of HC electrodes between ether-based (DM82, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4E,F</a>, Supporting Information) and ester-based (ED11, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4G,H</a>, Supporting Information) electrolyte systems. Therefore, high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) characterization was further conducted to reveal the fine structure of the HC particle surface. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0002">2A</a>, a much thinner and uniform SEI film was formed in DM82, and the occurrence of inorganic NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> was mainly distributed in the internal layer of SEI film. In distinct comparison, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0002">2B</a>, a very thick and irregular SEI film was produced in ED11, containing randomly mixed amorphous and crystalline regions through the whole thickness of SEI layer. The HRTEM image shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) exhibits that the thickness of SEI film formed on HC anode surface in D1 was also much thicker than that in DM82. In addition, the orgainc species were distributed widely over the full thickness of the SEI film, while the inorganic species, such as Na<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and NaF, were mainly distributed in the inner part of the SEI film. Furthermore, the kinetics of sodium ion storage in HC electrodeand the evolution of SEI film during cycling were investigated based on electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements within 18 cycles at a current density of 150&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Selected Nyquist plots for HC electrodes during EIS tests were displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0002">2C,D</a>, which exhibited two semi-circles in the high and medium frequency regions in both DM82 and ED11, revealing similar electrochemical processes in HC electrodes. Nevertheless, the overall resistance on HC electrodes in ED11 is larger than that in DM82. In order to further quantitatively investigate the electrochemical processes in HC electrodes, these EIS spectra were processed with distribution of relaxation time (DRT) analysis which plots the distribution of polarization loss versus time. In comparison with the traditional Nyquist plot of the EIS data, the DRT plot can provide direct timescale information for the main polarization processes occurring in the electrochemical systems. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0002">2E,F</a>, distinct peaks are classified based on time scales: electrolyte Ohmic resistance (R<sub>e</sub>, below 10<sup>−2</sup> s), SEI film resistance (R<sub>SEI</sub>, 10<sup>−2</sup>–1 s), charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>, 1–10<sup>2</sup> s). The center of the peak and the area under the peak correspond to the relaxation time and the resistance of the polarization process, respectively. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0002">2G,H</a>, for both electrolyte systems, R<sub>SEI</sub> and R<sub>ct</sub> dominate the cell resistances. With cycling prolonged, the SEI layer formed on HC in DM82 maintains a relatively low and stable value over 18 cycles, indicating a bearable SEI layer on HC electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0002">2G</a>), which is in line with the formation of thin and uniform SEI film shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0002">2A</a> accounting for the excellent rate kinetics and cycling stability of HC electrodes in DM82 shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0001">1E,F</a>. In contrast, relatively large R<sub>ct</sub> and continuously increasing R<sub>SEI</sub> values are observed for HC electrode in ED11 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0002">2H</a>), probably resulting from the dissolution of SEI layer components in ester-based electrolyte,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0024_R_d22396723e764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which leads to continuous formation of SEI layer, and thus higher SEI layer resistance as well as inferior rate and cycling performance of HC electrodes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202411531-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/362b7a4a-20f8-4569-a1f6-59997fe5e129/smll202411531-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/362b7a4a-20f8-4569-a1f6-59997fe5e129/smll202411531-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/49da6438-c9f0-4d0c-983f-e0bbd0ddbe19/smll202411531-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/362b7a4a-20f8-4569-a1f6-59997fe5e129/smll202411531-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411531-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">HRTEM images of SEI film on HC after 2 cycles at a current density of 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in DM82 A) and ED11 B), EIS spectra of HC electrodes with DM82 C) and ED11 D), and the corresponding DRT analysis E,F). The evolution of R<sub>e</sub>, R<sub>SEI</sub>, and R<sub>ct</sub> during cycling in DM82 G) and ED11 H).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Further, considering that the chemical composition and microstructure of SEI film play an important role in Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion process through SEI film and the subsequent charge transfer process, a systematic X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) characterization with different Ar<sup>+</sup> etching depth was performed to comprehensively investigate the dynamic evolutions of SEI films in different electrolyte systems and their influences on the dramatically different electrochemical performances, and the results are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0003">3</a>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0003">3A</a>, before Ar<sup>+</sup> etching, the C1s spectrum for HC electrode cycled in DM82 displays four fitted peaks located at 283.25, 284.8, 286.56, and 289.34&nbsp;eV. The peak at 283.25&nbsp;eV can be attributed to C in sodiated HC electrode (Na<sub>x</sub>- HC).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0025_R_d22396723e818" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C1s peaks at 284.8 (C-C/C-H), 286.56 (C-O) and O1s peak at 533.05 (C-O/O-H) eV can be ascribed to organic species formed by the reduction of organic solvent molecules in the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0026_R_d22396723e823" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The O1s peaks at 531.3 and 536.2&nbsp;eV can be assigned to the inorganic components composed of Na-O and Na-O-F, respectively, which were originated from NaPF<sub>6</sub>-solvent complex reductions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0027_R_d22396723e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The F1s peaks at 684.4 and 687.6&nbsp;eV corresponded to Na-F, Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub> and Na<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F, respectively, resulting from the reductive decomposition of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion solvates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0028_R_d22396723e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Cent. Sci.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is found that, along with the Ar<sup>+</sup> etching for 5 and 10&nbsp;nm, the intensities of C1s peaks at 286.56 (C-O) and 289.34 (C = O) eV decreased, while those of C1s peak at 283.25&nbsp;eV (Na<sub>x</sub>-HC) and F1s peak at 684.4&nbsp;eV (Na-F) obviously increased, indicating the formation of a thin SEI film with a layered structure on HC electrode cycled in DM82, which is in line the HRTEM result (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0002">2A</a>). The outer SEI layer composed of organic ingredients can ensure excellent chemical resistance toward organic solvents and good flexibility for enduring electrode deformation, while the inner inorganic-rich SEI layer guaranteed favorable mechanical strength of SEI film and unobstructed transportation path for sodium ions, which is important for HC electrodes to obtain superior rate capability and long-term cycling performance in DM82. Meanwhile, HC electrodes cycled in D1 and ED11, respectively, presented similar XPS peak shapes and positions to those in DM82, except for the disappearance of XPS peaks for Na<sub>x</sub>-HC (C1s at 283.25&nbsp;eV) which indicated the formation of a much thicker SEI film on HC electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0003">3B</a>), accounting for the inferior rate capability. In addition, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the intensities of the C1s peaks at 284.8 (C-C/C-H), 286.56 (C-O) eV and the O1s peak at 533.05 (C-O/O-H) eV representing the organic species are nearly the same before and after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching, while those of the O1s peaks at 531.3 (Na-O) and 536.2 (Na-O-F) eV, as well as the F1s peak at 684.4&nbsp;eV (Na-F) representing the inorganic species clearly increase after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching, which are in line with the HRTEM characterization (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0003">3B</a> shows that the intensities of C1s peaks at 284.8 (C-C/C-H) and 286.56 (C-O), as well as F1s peak at 684.4&nbsp;eV (Na-F) simultaneously increased after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching for 5 and 10&nbsp;nm, indicating that the SEI film on HC electrodes cycled in ED11 was composed randomly of organic and inorganic components, resulting from the overlapped reduction potentials for different solvated salts and solvents in ED11. Such a random distribution of inorganic and organic components could not effectively stabilize HC electrodes owing to the dissolution of inorganic SEI components into ester-based electrolyte system, leading to continuous SEI damage/repair during cycling, and thus inducing the formation of a thicker and thicker SEI film which is harmful for HC electrodes to obtain a good rate capability and cycling stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202411531-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/62a70bb2-0ea9-429a-9697-ed1bb3b78b88/smll202411531-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/62a70bb2-0ea9-429a-9697-ed1bb3b78b88/smll202411531-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d8a995ab-6205-42da-aacf-d3b3128f5d27/smll202411531-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/62a70bb2-0ea9-429a-9697-ed1bb3b78b88/smll202411531-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411531-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS measurements before and after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching for cycled (0.1 C, 2nd cycle, discharged state) HC electrodes in DM82 A) and ED11 B).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0004">4A</a> shows a photograph of the four kinds of electrolyte systems, revealing that these electrolyte systems are clarified and diffluent liquid, indicating sodium salt NaPF<sub>6</sub> can well dissolved in DME, MeTHF, and their mixture. As is well known, the rate performance of SIBs is significantly related to the sodium ion desolvation process at the electrode/electrolyte interface, which is determined by the inherent sodium ion solvation structure in electrolyte systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0029_R_d22396723e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the sodium ion solvation structures in different electrolyte systems were first investigated by classical molecular dynamics (CMD) simulations based on which the simulated snapshots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0004">4B–E</a>) of these electrolyte systems and their corresponding radial distribution function (RDF) curves as well as coordination number (CN) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0004">4F–I</a>) for the phosphorus (P) atoms in anions and oxygen (O) atoms in solvent molecules toward sodium ions can be obtained. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0004">4F,G</a> show the peaks of Na-O(DME/MeTHF) at ≈0.24&nbsp;nm and Na-P(PF<sub>6</sub>) at ≈0.33&nbsp;nm, revealing the coordination of Na<sup>+</sup> with DME/PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and MeTHF/ PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in the first solvation sheath in the single solvent electrolyte systems D1 and M1, respectively. The average Na-O and Na-P coordination numbers of D1 and M1 are 3.64/2.0 and 2.2/2.1, respectively. Considering that there are 2/1 oxygen atoms and 1 phosphorus atom in DME/MeTHF molecule and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion, respectively, it can be calculated that the average numbers of solvent molecules and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions involved in Na<sup>+</sup> solvation are 1.82/2.0 and 2.2/2.1 in D1 and M1, respectively. Similarly, the average CNs of DME/MeTHF and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions in Na<sup>+</sup> solvation sheath in DM82 are 1.9/0.4 and 1.4, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0003">3H</a>). Interestingly, although both DME and MeTHF molecules participate in the primary Na<sup>+</sup> solvation sheath in the electrolyte system DM82, the average CN of MeTHF (0.4) molecule is significantly smaller than DME (1.9) molecule, indicating a much weaker coordination ability toward Na<sup>+</sup> of MeTHF molecule than that of DME. For comparison, the average CNs of EC/DMC and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions in Na<sup>+</sup> solvation sheath in ED11 are 3.4/1.6 and 0.7, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0003">3I</a>), suggesting that the coordination ability of the linear carbonate ester solvent molecule (DMC) toward Na<sup>+</sup> is much weaker than that of the cyclic carbonate ester solvent molecule (EC). In order to verify the sodium ion solvation structure experimentally, Raman spectroscopy measurements were conducted for each electrolyte system and their components including NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt, DME, MeTHF, and DME/MeTHF solvents. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0004">4J</a>, the P-F stretching vibration peak of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> at 765&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> exhibits an obvious red shift to 742&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> upon dissolution into different solvents, suggesting a great disintegration of Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> interactions. Additionally, a new vibration peak at 863&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> appears in DME-based electrolyte systems, indicating the existence of Na<sup>+</sup>-DME complex. In comparison, the locations of vibration peaks at 923 and 813&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are almost identical for the pure MeTHF solvent and NaPF<sub>6</sub>-MeTHF (M1) electrolyte, implying a relatively weak interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> cation and MeTHF molecule. The mean square displacement (MSD) value is one of the most important dynamic results of the CMD simulation. MSD values of sodium ion in different electrolyte systems are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0004">4K</a>. It can be seen that the traversed distances in equal times for sodium ions, namely the diffusion coefficient of sodium ion, decrease sequentially in DM82, D1, M1, and ED11. In addition, the viscosity values can also be theoretically calculated based on the CMD simulation, and the results are 1.58, 2.33, 2.43, and 13.4 mPa&nbsp;s for electrolyte systems DM82, D1, M1, and ED11, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0004">4L</a>), which shows a same order of magnitude as the sodium ion diffusion coefficient, suggesting the low viscosity of the electrolyte system is beneficial for the sodium ion transportation in electrolyte systems, and thus improving the rate capability of the cells. These simulation results demonstrate an enhanced and optimal Na<sup>+</sup> ionic conductivity in single-ether- and binary-ether-based electrolyte system, respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202411531-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dd8ffb7c-53c4-4dee-98d3-20a879ccb1b4/smll202411531-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dd8ffb7c-53c4-4dee-98d3-20a879ccb1b4/smll202411531-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a145f478-bf44-414c-9a91-0a81cc7b7448/smll202411531-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dd8ffb7c-53c4-4dee-98d3-20a879ccb1b4/smll202411531-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411531-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Optical images of prepared different electrolyte systems A). CMD simulation snapshots for different electrolyte systems B–E). Color code: H, grey, C, green, O, red, F, brown, Na, blue, P, olive. RDFs and the corresponding CNs of Na<sup>+</sup>-P (PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>) and Na<sup>+</sup>-O (solvent) in different electrolyte systems F–I). Raman spectra of pure NaPF<sub>6</sub> powders, D1, M1, and DM82 solvents and their corresponding electrolyte systems D1, M1, and DM82 J). MSD of sodium ion versus time K), theoretically calculated viscosity values L) for different electrolyte systems.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Based on the CMD simulation results of RDF and CN, Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-anion and Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent (solvent = DME, MeTHF, DME/MeTHF and EC/DMC) complexes were modeled and optimized at the B3LYP/6-311G* level of theory, and then the single-point energies of the optimized solvation structures, as well as the isolated ions (Na<sup>+</sup> and PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>) and solvent molecules (DME, MeTHF, EC, and DMC), for each electrolyte system were calculated based on density functional theory (DFT) method, yielding calculated values of sodium ion desolvation energy barriers (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5A</a>) and optimized sodium ion solvation structures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5B–E</a>). The details of these DFT calculations can be found in the supporting information of this article. It is shown that the sodium ion desolvation energy barriers are 36.13, 24.86, 11.54, and 69.17&nbsp;kcal mol<sup>−1</sup> for electrolyte systems D1, M1, DM82, and ED11, respectively, revealing that the participation of MeTHF molecule with weak coordination ability toward Na<sup>+</sup> in sodium ion solvation sheath significantly decreases the sodium ion desolvation energy barrier. A much higher sodium ion desolvation energy barrier in electrolyte system ED11 indicates that the ester solvents present much stronger coordination with Na<sup>+</sup> than the ether solvents, which is due to the fact that the carbonyl oxygen exhibits higher nucleophilicity than the ethereal oxygen, leading to a higher sodium ion desolvation energy barrier. Furthermore, it is well-known that, besides the above-mentioned CN values, the average bond length is another important parameter to describe the geometrical arrangement of solvent molecules/anions surrounding the cation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0030_R_d22396723e1071" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Sci.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5B–E</a>, the favorable sites for interaction of Na<sup>+</sup> is near the ethereal oxygen atoms and fluoride atoms, and carbonyl oxygen atoms in the ether- and ester-based electrolyte systems, respectively. The basic solvation geometry around Na<sup>+</sup> among the four complexes is different in the solvation sheaths. Specifically, there are two DME molecules and two PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, two MeTHF molecules and two PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, two DME molecule, one MeTHF molecule and two PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, and four EC molecules and two DMC molecules in the solvation sheath for D1, M1, DM82, and ED11, respectively. It is worthy of noting that both the weak-coordinating MeTHF molecule and the strong-coordinating DME molecule are involved in the first solvation sheath of sodium ion. This observation can be explained as follows. The coordination ability of solvent molecules with ions is determined by their distinct structural features including polarity, steric hindrance, and denticity. DME has much stronger solvating power toward sodium ion than that of MeTHF owing to its higher polarity and bidentate nature. The two oxygen atoms of DME molecule are probably binding to the same sodium ion. If each oxygen atom of DME molecule is regarded as one ligand, the adjusted coordination ability of DME molecule is close to that of MeTHF molecule with only one oxygen atom binding to sodium ion. Hence, MeTHF molecule could be involved in the first solvation sheath of sodium ion along with DME molecule with only one oxygen atom binding to sodium ion, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5D</a>. In addition, the average Na<sup>+</sup>-O/F bond lengths in Na<sup>+</sup>(DME)<sub>2</sub>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)<sub>2</sub>, Na<sup>+</sup>(MeTHF)<sub>2</sub>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)<sub>2</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup>(DME)<sub>2</sub>(MeTHF)(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)<sub>2</sub> complexes are 2.37/2.32, 2.27/2.38, and 2.38/2.30 Å, respectively, while the average Na<sup>+</sup>-O bond length in Na<sup>+</sup>(EC)<sub>4</sub>(DMC)<sub>2</sub> is 2.39 Å. The smaller Na<sup>+</sup>-O bond length in Na<sup>+</sup>(MeTHF)<sub>2</sub>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)<sub>2</sub> compared to that in Na<sup>+</sup>(DME)<sub>2</sub>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)<sub>2</sub> can be ascribed to a smaller size of MeTHF molecule compared to that of DME molecule, which means a smaller steric hindrance. It is also found that the average Na<sup>+</sup>-O bond length increases with the addition of MeTHF solvent in electrolyte system, indicating a weakening of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent interaction. In order to unravel the origin of the molecular interactions in Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complexes, the independent gradient model based on Hirshfeld partition (IGMH)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0031_R_d22396723e1170" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Comput. Chem.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was employed to investigate the non-covalent interactions (NCIs) by using the Multiwfn program.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0032_R_d22396723e1176" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Comput. Chem.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The calculated interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvent/PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> were visualized by using the VMD software,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0033_R_d22396723e1186" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Mol. Graph.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5F–I</a>. There are six green round isosurfaces between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvent/PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, which represent strong ionic interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and negatively charged O/F atoms. Meanwhile, the bond critical points (BCPs) and bond paths generated by atom-in-molecules (AIM) topology analysis for Na<sup>+</sup>-O/F bonds are also shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5F–I</a>. AIM theory was introduced by Bader who pointed out that the essence of a molecule's structure should be included within the topology of electron density, denoted ρ(r), and resorted to mathematical tools to not only illustrate the properties of chemical systems but also extract visible information from electron density. Based on this mechanism, it is probable to relate topological properties of ρ(r) with elements of molecular structure, namely atoms and bonds, which enables quantum chemistry concepts compatible with classic chemical ideas. The total ρ(r) can be calculated from the wavefunction of the equilibrium geometry of various species. The topological properties of ρ(r) are characterized by its critical points where ▽ρ(r) = 0. As the electron density is at a maximal at the nuclei, the localization of maxima makes the identification of atomic positions. When atoms are identified, chemical bonds could be defined as saddle points between these maxima. These saddle points are regarded as BCPs and denote the minimum along the bonding direction. The presence of a BCP between two atoms is a universal indicator of bonded interactions and the electron density at the BCP (ρ<sup>BCP</sup>) is related to the bond strength, while a bond path is generated by tracing the steepest ascent path from a BCP, which could be seen as the most representative path displaying an interatomic interaction. Therefore, the IGMH map complemented by AIM topology analysis clearly reveal that the interaction in Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> complexes can be attributed to the interatomic interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and O/F atoms. Furthermore, the ρ<sup>BCP</sup> for Na<sup>+</sup>-O interaction is stronger than that for Na<sup>+</sup>-F interaction in Na<sup>+</sup>-DME-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>-MeTHF-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> complexes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5F,G</a>). In other words, the Na<sup>+</sup>-O interaction dominates the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent/PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> interaction in the single solvent-based electrolyte systems D1 and M1. For the binary solvent-based electrolyte system DM82, the ρ<sup>BCP</sup> for Na<sup>+</sup>-O (MeTHF) interaction is larger than those for both Na<sup>+</sup>-O (DME) and Na<sup>+</sup>-F interactions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5H</a>). For the ester-based electrolyte system ED11, ρ<sup>BCP</sup> values for Na<sup>+</sup>-O (EC) and Na<sup>+</sup>-O (DMC) interactions are on a level (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5I</a>). Furthermore, comparing the ρ<sup>BCP</sup> values in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5F</a> with those in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0005">5H</a>, it is found that the ρ<sup>BCP</sup> values for both Na<sup>+</sup>-O (DME) and Na<sup>+</sup>-F interactions decrease in DM82 than those in D1, indicating the existence of Na<sup>+</sup>-O (MeTHF) interaction significantly weakens the interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and DME/PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. Although the ρ<sup>BCP</sup> for Na<sup>+</sup>-O (MeTHF) is predominant, the MeTHF is intrinsically a weak coordination solvent toward Na<sup>+</sup> due to its single coordination site, resulting in a low binding energy in the Na<sup>+</sup>-DME/MeTHF-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> complex, and leading to a low desolvation energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, and thus enabling high-rate HC electrodes. It appears that concentrating electron densities at BCPs between Na<sup>+</sup> and weak-coordinating solvent molecules rather than those at BCPs between Na<sup>+</sup> and strong-coordinating solvent molecules or sodium salt anions is beneficial for Na<sup>+</sup> transportation kinetics. Therefore, it is inferred that the electron density engineering at the BCPs in solvation sheath of sodium ions plays an important role in regulating the transportation behaviors of sodium ions, and thus boosting the electrochemical performance, especially the rate capability, of HC electrodes in ether-based electrolyte systems composed of strong-coordinating and weak-coordinating solvent molecules at a specific ratio.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202411531-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/521c7974-caaa-4ff3-b713-2e960dd52441/smll202411531-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/521c7974-caaa-4ff3-b713-2e960dd52441/smll202411531-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aa613001-7aea-4c34-90bd-a249cd954dec/smll202411531-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/521c7974-caaa-4ff3-b713-2e960dd52441/smll202411531-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411531-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium ion desolvation energy barriers in electrolyte systems D1, M1, DM82, and ED11 A). Geometries of the first solvation sheath of Na<sup>+</sup> in electrolyte systems D1 B), M1 C), DM82 D), and ED11 E), respectively. The numbers in these graphs represent bond lengths. IGMH maps with BCPs (orange spheres) and bond paths (yellow lines) of Na<sup>+</sup>-DME-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> F), Na<sup>+</sup>-MeTHF-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> G), Na<sup>+</sup>-DME/MeTHF-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> H) and Na<sup>+</sup>-EC/DMC I) complexes, respectively. The numbers in these graphs represent electron densities at the corresponding BCPs (ρ<sup>BCP</sup>).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In order to investigate the electrochemical performance of electrolyte system DM82 in full cells, HC|NaCrO<sub>2</sub> coin cells were assembled with a HC and NaCrO<sub>2</sub> loading of about 2.1 and 2.5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively, using DM82 and ED11 for comparison, respectively, as the electrolyte systems. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0006">6A</a> shows the initial charge/discharge profiles of these cells in the voltage range of 1.5-3.4&nbsp;V. The initial capacities were ≈104.86 and 81.75 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for the electrolyte system DM82 and ED11, respectively. The rate capability and cycling performance of cells with electrolyte system ED11 were disappointing (41.79% at the 200 cycle) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0006">6C</a>). In contrast, the capacity retention of cells with electrolyte system DM82 was ≈76.83% after 200 cycles, displaying a CE of ≈98% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0006">6C</a>). In addition, a superior rate capability can be observed for full cells with DM82 in comparison with those with ED11 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0006">6B</a>). It should be noted that the rate capability of full cells was also determined by the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> cathode as well as the electrode thickness, and thus further optimization of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> cathode, such as carbon coating,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202411531-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202411531-bib-0034_R_d22396723e1385" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Environ. Sci.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the thickness of cathode/anode is also important for boosting the rate capability of full cells. These electrochemical results further demonstrate the effectiveness of the ether-based electrolyte system DM82 in improving rate capability and cycling performance of HC-based SIBs. Therefore, we demonstrate herein that an ether-based electrolyte system composed of 1&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a mixed solvent system of DME and MeTHF with a molar ratio of 8:2, namely DM82, for use in SIBs. This electrolyte system can stabilize the electrode-electrolyte interphase for HC electrodes, enabling high rate performance of HC electrodes and enhanced cycling performance of SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202411531-fig-0006">6D</a>). It is worthy to mention that further investigation is still needed in order to improve the compatibility of this kind of ether-based electrolyte system with high-voltage cathode materials for enabling simultaneously high-energy and high-rate SIBs, which is beyond the scope of this study.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202411531-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b32473e3-0a0f-4eab-93f8-18419118e020/smll202411531-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b32473e3-0a0f-4eab-93f8-18419118e020/smll202411531-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5a9d558e-de0d-4062-a02a-6f1790441a73/smll202411531-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b32473e3-0a0f-4eab-93f8-18419118e020/smll202411531-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202411531-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202411531" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">GCD profiles of SIBs at a current density of 12.5&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> from 1.5 to 3.4&nbsp;V at room temperature with electrolyte systems DM82 and ED11, respectively A). Rate capability at various C-rates B). Cycling performance of full cells at 1 C C). Schematic illustration of an HC||DM82||NaCrO<sub>2</sub> full cell D).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202411531-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202411531-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have designed an ether-based electrolyte system composed of 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a solvent mixture of DME and MeTHF with an optimal molar ratio of 8:2, which is denoted as DM82, to enable high-rate HC electrodes. AIM topology analysis clearly reveal that the electron densities at BCPs in the sodium ion solvation sheath play an important role in regulating the NCIs in Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> complexes for accelerating Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation. Additionally, the as-designed electrolyte system presents high sodium ion diffusion coefficient and low viscosity. Taking these advantages together, a HC||DM82||NaCrO<sub>2</sub> full cell exhibited drastically enhanced electrochemical performance in terms of rate capability and cycling stability, in comparison with the cells using a commercially available ester-based electrolyte system. The concept of electron density engineering at BCPs in solvation sheath of sodium ions provides a novel design philosophy for new electrolyte systems toward high rate and long-life SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202411531-sec-0040">
